<div id="review_2920184307" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2920184307" />
    <a title="Lauren Stoolfire" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6857324-lauren-stoolfire"><img alt="Lauren Stoolfire" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1595823149p2/6857324.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2920184307?book_show_action=true">Jun 13, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lauren Stoolfire" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lauren Stoolfire" href="/user/show/6857324-lauren-stoolfire">Lauren Stoolfire</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6857324-lauren-stoolfire?shelf=dystopia">dystopia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6857324-lauren-stoolfire?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6857324-lauren-stoolfire?shelf=lgbtq">lgbtq</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2920184307" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3569672457011188573"><i>Wraeththu</i> by Storm Constantine has an intriguing premise featuring a new type of humanity that has evolved in Earth's future that will replace Homo sapiens as the dominant species. They have masculine and feminine traits and have some psychic and magical abilities. People can either be born Wraeththu (pronounced Ray-thoo) or you can undergo the magical rituals to ascend to Wraeththu. Pellaz is one of those second types. I was actually quite interested in following Pellaz's spiritual journey into</span>
  <span id="freeText3569672457011188573" style="display:none"><i>Wraeththu</i> by Storm Constantine has an intriguing premise featuring a new type of humanity that has evolved in Earth's future that will replace Homo sapiens as the dominant species. They have masculine and feminine traits and have some psychic and magical abilities. People can either be born Wraeththu (pronounced Ray-thoo) or you can undergo the magical rituals to ascend to Wraeththu. Pellaz is one of those second types. I was actually quite interested in following Pellaz's spiritual journey into the world of the har (what the Wraeththu call themselves) and becoming one himself, especially in book one in this collected trilogy. I was glad to see that there is a glossary since there a so many new words that Constantine introduces in this series. I probably just should've stopped there though because afterwards it all starts to go downhill. To be honest, there's not a whole lot of plot to this trilogy and the dialogue is a bit of a nightmare at times. 213 pages was fine for the first book, but 800 pages for the whole trilogy is really pushing it to its limits. I was especially intrigued by how this story would discuss gender, but that was a colossal disappointment and don't get me started on everything to do with aruna. Then there's Cal who went from being okay I guess to a figure I totally hated. I definitely should have just stopped after I completed book one (<i>The Enchantments of Flesh and Spirit</i>), and just skipped book two (<i>The Bewitchments of Love and Hate</i>) and book three (<i>The Fulfillments of Fate and Desire</i>) all together.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3569672457011188573" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2857615296" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2857615296" />
    <a title="Mata" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/98522012-mata"><img alt="Mata" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2857615296?book_show_action=true">Jun 14, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mata" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mata" href="/user/show/98522012-mata">Mata</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/98522012-mata?shelf=lgbt-scifi">lgbt-scifi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/98522012-mata?shelf=masochistic-read">masochistic-read</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2857615296" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3368526780991344353">This book caused me a lot of <i>grief</i> when I first came across it in my teens. Grief as in the book and its setting was so wonderfully written, but while Storm has a way with words, some of her characters got me so depressed that I refused to read the second book in the trilogy (or merely skipped it until recently).<br/><br/>Let's start from the beginning. <br/><br/><b>Book 1</b><br/><br/>The most intriguing book since it explores the origins of Wraeththu from the eyes of Pell, a stunningly beautiful peasant boy, living in a desert fa</span>
  <span id="freeText3368526780991344353" style="display:none">This book caused me a lot of <i>grief</i> when I first came across it in my teens. Grief as in the book and its setting was so wonderfully written, but while Storm has a way with words, some of her characters got me so depressed that I refused to read the second book in the trilogy (or merely skipped it until recently).<br /><br />Let's start from the beginning. <br /><br /><b>Book 1</b><br /><br />The most intriguing book since it explores the origins of Wraeththu from the eyes of Pell, a stunningly beautiful peasant boy, living in a desert far, far away from the wretchedness of the desolated cities in what used to be the United States of America (set in a dystopian future, kinda MaxMaxish). Where he lives is so isolated that he and his family only hear bits of what's happening from travellers who've left those cities for an uncertain future. <br /><br />One thing they all warn though is that a strange something has corrupted beautiful young boys back in those cities and they have now evolved into something not really human but a mixture of male and female (what we, the readers, find out are Wraeththu later on). The travellers warn Pell and his family that those troubled boys might even pass by the desert and kidnap them, which they laugh off...until it does happen in the form of a beguiling blond wanderer named Calanthe. <br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Pell gets incepted into Wraeththu and he and Cal get up to a lot of adventures and travels in Pell's quest to gain spiritual and magical training. Along the way, they meet bewitching (and some irritating) characters who either train Pell magically or just want to get in their pants. At one point, they stay in a picturesque town after rescuing another unfortunate character who's actually the consort to some really powerful dude named Terzian (who we will see much more in Book 2), but yikes, Terzian manages to falls in love with Cal after just a day of talking to him. (Laughable really but what the hell, the book has hermaphrodites, magic and even technology, but for some reason, they can't really drive cars because fuel has become hard to find). In the end, Cal ultimately rejects him (not before pondering about it for a good second, no doubt) because he has fallen in love with Pell and they leave before shit gets down.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br /><b>Book 2: Told in the POV of Swift, the son of Terzian and Cobweb.</b><br /><br />This book was painful to read. Storm is such a powerful writer that she managed to make me cry over the mistreatment of beautiful Cobweb, Terzian's consort and the mother (hostling) of Swift. I skipped this story when I was younger because when I found out that Cal came back and both he and Terzian humiliated Cobweb just to get into Cal's pants, I just wished them both dead. <br />I only managed to fully read it nearly 15 years later and it's still painful to digest but at least I got over the trauma (still not over my hatred for Terzian though). Won’t spoil it much for you but it’s kinda depressing to find out that other hars were torturing their own kind for power and conquest.<br /> <br /><b>Book 3: Told in the POV of Cal, who now has to redeem himself in order to make his way back to Pell. </b><br /><br />The most irritating book of all because I loathed Cal with a vengeance (which took me years to get over). This chronicles his journey to redemption and the numerous challenges he had to overcome after he finally, FINALLY accepted that he needs to get over his bullshit to reach his destiny: Pell. However, this book had more lost souls falling in love with Cal again. <br /><br />So yeah, it took me over 10 years to properly read the book because of one character - Cal. Motherfucking Calanthe, who is beloved or lusted over by all. When reading the books you'd probably think Cal is the most attractive har in the whole of Wraeththu because everyone wants to instantly sleep with him or fall in love with him *gag*. (I kinda saw the appeal after I saw the 2018 cover of "The Fulfilments of Fate and Desire" where we can picture how Cal actually looks like but come on). <br /><br />I still don't understand Storm's obsession with Cal to make him so godlike that every being who came across him or his luscious bod obsessed over him too. Kinda reminded me of those Mary Stu/Gary Stu/Sailor Moon type of character that is irresistible to all (which was a shame because there were so many brilliant characters in the whole book that should have been fleshed out more). <br /><br />I, for one, loved Pell so was glad that his character is more fleshed out in the second trilogy of the Wraeththu mythos. Good thing is that Cal does redeem himself in my eyes, particularly the second book called "The Shades of Time and Memory"; a brilliant read!</span>
  <a data-text-id="3368526780991344353" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer1099398929" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11055102178635522342"><i>Full disclosure: I read the entirety of the collection's first book, "</i>The Enchantments of Flesh and Spirit<i>" and some (I would say most) of the second. </i><br/><br/>Oh man, Wraeththu. Let's talk about Wraeththu.<br/><br/>When I was younger, I saw this book all the time when browsing the science-fiction section of Barnes & Noble. It called out with its bizarre cover art and weird title (it looks like Cthulhu, so it must be Lovecraftian, right?), and every time I'd put it back on the shelf after checking a description on</span>
  <span id="freeText11055102178635522342" style="display:none"><i>Full disclosure: I read the entirety of the collection's first book, "</i>The Enchantments of Flesh and Spirit<i>" and some (I would say most) of the second. </i><br /><br />Oh man, Wraeththu. Let's talk about Wraeththu.<br /><br />When I was younger, I saw this book all the time when browsing the science-fiction section of Barnes &amp; Noble. It called out with its bizarre cover art and weird title (it looks like Cthulhu, so it must be Lovecraftian, right?), and every time I'd put it back on the shelf after checking a description on the back that even preteen me could read between the lines and realize: this book is about <i>gay</i> people. Shudder. Anyway, now that I'm older (and mature enough to not find the idea of two dudes kissing gross - what an asshole I was) I actively seek out LGBT sci-fi, and I started thinking about Wraeththu again. I wonder what that weird-ass book was like? Surely it couldn't be as bad as I imagined?<br /><br />Well, Constantine's Wraeththu novels are definitely the kind of books that would've blown the mind of eleven-year-old Greg. That said, they're not exactly <i>good</i>.<br /><br />Storm Constantine (who by the way is a gothy-looking British lady, not the Native American chieftain I always imagined) has come up with an interesting conceit: the world of men is in a slow, cold war with a new species on the fringe of society called Wraeththu. Rumors about them abound; some swear they're just a confederation of street gangs bent on revolution, some people think they're radioactive mutants. A young man living out in the boonies named Pellaz finds himself obsessed with finding out everything he can about the enigmatic creatures, and when a strange wounded man named Cal arrives at his parents' farm claiming to <i>be</i> one of these fantastic monsters in disguise, Pellaz runs away from home, accompanying Cal on a journey to beome Wraeththu himself and understand his nascent sexuality. This part of the book is actually cool and good - I liked Pellaz's transition from snarky brat to world-weary traveler, and the first 1/3rd of the book (which ends with Pellaz undergoing the arcane rituals to become a Wraeththu himself) make everything sound really creepy and mysterious. It's what comes after that poses problems.<br /><br />Make no mistake: the back of the book might call them hermaphrodites, but 'Wraeththu' is totally code for 'fabulous gay man'. Every Wraeththu Pell meets is impossibly suave and beautiful, with perfect skin and long hair like an anime bishounen. The way they dress causes Constantine to overuse the word 'diaphenous' like no other author I've ever read. All of them, particularly Cal, detest the "female essence" within them and enhance their innerselves via sex magic and magic sex (two <i>completely different things</i>, by the way) through "aruna," which involves putting your Wraeththu genitals (which look like a magic penis flower) into another Wraeththu's genitals like those things the Na'vi have in <i>Avatar</i>. It's as strange as it sounds.<br /><br />None of this is actually a problem for me; I've read Delany for chrissakes (the really <i>bad</i> Delany, mind). It's that the rest of the book breaks down like this:<br /><br />1) Pell and Cal wander into another town<br />2) A hunky, diaphenous Wraeththu wants to <s>have passionate gay sex</s> 'share aruna' with Cal/Pell<br />3) They do, it makes Pell/Cal jealous, they fight about it for a bit<br />4) They make up and move onto another town<br /><br />Calling it plotless would be generous. Neither character seems to have any real motivation: Pell wants to become a higher-level Wraeththu until he suddenly doesn't, he wants to meet someone who treats him better than Cal until he doesn't. Cal wants to wander around until he finds the legendary Wraeththu city of Immanion, for reasons we're never given, Until he doesn't. Until he does. If you think the characters are inconsistent, you should read the goddamn dialog: characters spend whole PARAGRAPHS conversing in prose flowery enough to leave a Tolkien elf going "okay, you're overdoing it a little" then turn around and say "That's bullshit, you're just lazy as fuck" like it ain't no thing. Even the setting can't decide whether its the 1960's or 20XX. <br /><br />I got to the second book in the trilogy, discovered it to be the exact same odyssey-of-sexual-self-discovery plot as the first, read most of it, and decided the rest of the book just wasn't for me. There's lots of great queer science-fiction out there, but Wraeththu isn't it. Sometimes your eleven year-old self had the right idea all along.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11055102178635522342" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Aaron Carson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Aaron Carson" href="/user/show/3171523-aaron-carson">Aaron Carson</a>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer250436239" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18292311187991833879">When I was younger, probably up to the age of sixteen. I read avidly, and lived with every character I read about, no matter how boring, or how badly written the book. When I turned seventeen I went through a change. It became increasingly hard for me to visualise what I was reading about, or feel anything about it. If anything I read even more avidly, but it was all a blur. This apathy leaked over into my daily life, and I began to find everything tasteless and predictable. <br/><br/>This book changed al</span>
  <span id="freeText18292311187991833879" style="display:none">When I was younger, probably up to the age of sixteen. I read avidly, and lived with every character I read about, no matter how boring, or how badly written the book. When I turned seventeen I went through a change. It became increasingly hard for me to visualise what I was reading about, or feel anything about it. If anything I read even more avidly, but it was all a blur. This apathy leaked over into my daily life, and I began to find everything tasteless and predictable. <br><br>This book changed all that. As soon as I started reading it, it began haunting me. It was a world combined out of everything I'd ever found enchanting, mysterious, and dangerous, in life, or in fiction. I literally couldn't put it down, and I don't often say that. I usually don't put books down, but normally this is a matter of discipline, not compulsion. <br><br>After I finished it I lent it to a friend of mine, who read it happily enough, but I was scandalized when she dubbed it a "gay fantasy." "Sacrilege!" I wanted to scream, but beauty is in the eye of the beholder. <br><br>One of the things I found so compelling about it was that it was definitely NOT a "Gay Fantasy," even though, the uninitiated, may see it as such. It was so much more subtle and profound than that. <br><br>For me, this book was amazing, because it transcended the usual limitations of high fantasy and low fantasy. It was older than ancient, and more futuristic than the apocalypse. It was desolate, mysterious, and filled with yearning, and solace. It evoked atmospheres of Egypt, the Inca, and even India, while never seeming to draw obviously from any of these traditions. It simply wasn't earthly, and yet it captured every scrap of magic, I've ever experienced on earth. <br><br>After such a rave review, I should probably point out, that the other books in the Wraeththu series are really only for die hard fans. This one stands out as unique and noble. The others, begin to flag, and become predictable, but if you really like this one, such volumes as<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/176713.The_Ghosts_of_Blood_and_Innocence__Wraeththu_Histories___3_" title="The Ghosts of Blood and Innocence (Wraeththu Histories, #3) by Storm Constantine" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/books/1316729545s/176713.jpg" alt="The Ghosts of Blood and Innocence (Wraeththu Histories, #3) by Storm Constantine" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/176712.The_Shades_of_Time_and_Memory__Wraeththu_Histories___2_" title="The Shades of Time and Memory (Wraeththu Histories, #2) by Storm Constantine" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/books/1312020002s/176712.jpg" alt="The Shades of Time and Memory (Wraeththu Histories, #2) by Storm Constantine" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a> May also inspire. </span>

<div id="review_33212986" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/33212986" />
    <a title="LKM" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1533779-lkm"><img alt="LKM" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1373646691p2/1533779.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/33212986?book_show_action=true">Sep 18, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="LKM" class="user" itemprop="url" name="LKM" href="/user/show/1533779-lkm">LKM</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1533779-lkm?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer33212986&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer33212986" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3259003830038285647">What can I say about this book? Ah! It took me long, very long to get through it, because the first book had not ended too well -or should I said, had a confusing ending (was it happy or sad...?), and I'm a sucker for happy endings.<br/><br/><br/>I think I'll start by pointing out I truly did like this book. Had a hard time deciding between four and five stars- I wanted four and a half, really.<br/><br/>The story on itself was great, fantastic in writing, the setting- not really something completely new and out of the o</span>
  <span id="freeText3259003830038285647" style="display:none">What can I say about this book? Ah! It took me long, very long to get through it, because the first book had not ended too well -or should I said, had a confusing ending (was it happy or sad...?), and I'm a sucker for happy endings.<br /><br /><br />I think I'll start by pointing out I truly did like this book. Had a hard time deciding between four and five stars- I wanted four and a half, really.<br /><br />The story on itself was great, fantastic in writing, the setting- not really something completely new and out of the ordinary, but interesting to read; the characters were all very interesting and deeply thought, though I could only get myself to truly care for two, and they were not even the main characters. <br />I was very interested at the subject of hermaphrodites, though I must say, I got a little tired of being told each har they met was super pretty and perfect- yes, we get it, all har are beautiful and perfect!<br /><br />I didn't really fancy the whole pregnancy thing, perhaps because I still saw them as very male-like, and as such, it all reeked of "mpreg".<br /><br />Two things threw me off the book, but I guess in the overall, they weren't so annoying that it made me dislike it entirely. (I mean, I did read it through and liked it plenty, after all)<br />The first was that the Wraeththu were a "very sexual race" which means, put plainly, "I love YOU, but I'll have sex with just about everyone I come across."<br />Sorry, but I like my romance romantic and faithful.<br /><br />The second was the reiteration of the ambiguous endings. <br />Were they happy? Were they sad? I can't decide!! It's driving me insane! The second book was the one that ended the best, and the third book, gosh, guess it should have been a happy ending, yet it was sad in a way. Ah, bittersweet, like life itself, I guess? I find myself torn to think on whether I enjoyed the ending... In a way, I think I didn't, I feel it should've developed a bit differently- I feel Calanthe should have said, "to hell! Pell, I love you, but it can't be." and started a whole new life. <br /><br />Ah well, I enjoyed the book, overall; though I had some visualization problems.</span>

<div id="review_286137654" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/286137654" />
    <a title="Marri" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4821805-marri"><img alt="Marri" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/286137654?book_show_action=true">Jan 17, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Marri" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Marri" href="/user/show/4821805-marri">Marri</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4821805-marri?shelf=post-apocalyptic">post-apocalyptic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4821805-marri?shelf=queer">queer</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4821805-marri?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4821805-marri?shelf=gender">gender</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4821805-marri?shelf=romance">romance</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4821805-marri?shelf=occult">occult</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer286137654" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8131183888326414851"><br/>I have a feeling I'll be digesting this one for a while. <br/><br/>The short version: The first book held a lot of promise that dissolved by the third book, with the second book being mostly a detour. There are some "revelations" in the third book that occur in the last 50 pages or so which are completely undersold by the 500+ pages beforehand. The ending was disappointingly conflict-free, with a few weird elements that felt last-minute or which raised more questions than they settled prior plot points, a</span>
  <span id="freeText8131183888326414851" style="display:none"><br />I have a feeling I'll be digesting this one for a while. <br /><br />The short version: The first book held a lot of promise that dissolved by the third book, with the second book being mostly a detour. There are some "revelations" in the third book that occur in the last 50 pages or so which are completely undersold by the 500+ pages beforehand. The ending was disappointingly conflict-free, with a few weird elements that felt last-minute or which raised more questions than they settled prior plot points, and somewhat predictable to those who understand neopagan concepts of gender and balance. <br /><br />The longer version: The premise is weird and original: a mutation causes men to become hermaphroditic while opening access to a host of spiritual powers that closely follow a neopagan cosmology. The unlocking of occult power or the transformation of human gender concepts are, individually, rich enough for whole novels. Somehow with them both sharing space here I don't feel we get to dive as deeply into either.<br /><br />The character motivation is highly melodramatic in the vein of Anne Rice; when it's successful in a few places, it pops, but when it flops or tries to support the whole plot--as in the entirety of the third book--it goes from charming to untenable. (The series was described as "Fanfictiony" to me and it certainly applies, in all the best and worst ways.) <br /><br />The large cast is a sea of beautifully unattainable promiscuous androgynes identifiable mostly by hair-color and affiliation, and I found myself confusing them a few times. The one character I became most curious about, Thiede, is held furthest away from us, and the main character of the first book is a shadow in the second and third books, with no main character strong enough to replace him. <br /><br />I expected a lot from this series because of its fascinating premise. The first book mostly delivered, but the second and third fizzled for me. <br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="8131183888326414851" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/732709-mlady-rebecca?shelf=2009">2009</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/732709-mlady-rebecca?shelf=fictional_series__traditional">fictional_series__traditional</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/732709-mlady-rebecca?shelf=fiction__speculative">fiction__speculative</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/732709-mlady-rebecca?shelf=omnibus_editions_and_sets">omnibus_editions_and_sets</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/732709-mlady-rebecca?shelf=genre_sub__dystopias_utopias">genre_sub__dystopias_utopias</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/732709-mlady-rebecca?shelf=genre__epic_fantasy">genre__epic_fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/732709-mlady-rebecca?shelf=author__storm_constantine">author__storm_constantine</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer27278329" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16996887007307722345">I want to call this a dystopia or an apocalyptic novel, but I'm not sure that it fits <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/topic/show/21923.Surprising_' rel='nofollow noopener'>either definition</a>. For man, it is certainly a cautionary tale, but it's not the story of man. It's the story of the Wraeththu, who evolve from and are torn from the last generation of man. It's a bloody rebirth or step in evolution. A chance to re-invent what is the best and worst of human, no harish, nature. <br/><br/>This was an omnibus edition of the first three books of the Wraeththu, but I can't imagine reading them</span>
  <span id="freeText16996887007307722345" style="display:none">I want to call this a dystopia or an apocalyptic novel, but I'm not sure that it fits <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/topic/show/21923.Surprising_" rel="nofollow noopener">either definition</a>. For man, it is certainly a cautionary tale, but it's not the story of man. It's the story of the Wraeththu, who evolve from and are torn from the last generation of man. It's a bloody rebirth or step in evolution. A chance to re-invent what is the best and worst of human, no harish, nature. <br /><br />This was an omnibus edition of the first three books of the Wraeththu, but I can't imagine reading them separately. They are not continuations, but parts of a whole. The story of three different hars and the role they are to play in the new civilization being built.<br /><br />It's a very character centric story, where minor characters who come and go manage to come around again at the end. Everything is circular, and it's a fascinating journey from the initial days of discord to the initial days of new balance.<br /><br />This may be portrayed as a gay story in reviews. It is, and it isn't. The wraeththu are both male and female, capable of giving or receiving during sex, capable of sparking life in another or giving birth in a whole new way. But the first generation of hars were converted from male, not female, humans. And part of that mindset stays behind. Not to mention the male pronoun. But "he" is not simply a "he". *g*<br /><br />Excellent trilogy. A bit slow to start, but that's true of many "whole new universe" style fantasies. I'll be searching out the Wreaththu Histories the next time I go on a book run. But maybe I should let the story sit for awhile first. The trilogy ends on a nice complete note. <br /><br />X-posted to <a href="http://mlady-rebecca.livejournal.com/438525.html" rel="nofollow noopener">LJ</a>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16996887007307722345" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2152211151" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2152211151" />
    <a title="Alina" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/23738466-alina"><img alt="Alina" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1379963649p2/23738466.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2152211151?book_show_action=true">Oct 31, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alina" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alina" href="/user/show/23738466-alina">Alina</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2152211151" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2843549426098875456">Two stars because in spite of all my thwarted expectations... I plowed through to the end of this series with some residual interest for the conclusion left to drive me. I've wanted to read these books since forever but somehow never got around to it and now... with years of expectations built up on sand... I can finally put the series with the impossible to remember name to rest. <br/><br/>Did I enjoy it? Not really. The premise was great. The idea worthy of exploring... it could have been amazing in eve</span>
  <span id="freeText2843549426098875456" style="display:none">Two stars because in spite of all my thwarted expectations... I plowed through to the end of this series with some residual interest for the conclusion left to drive me. I've wanted to read these books since forever but somehow never got around to it and now... with years of expectations built up on sand... I can finally put the series with the impossible to remember name to rest. <br /><br />Did I enjoy it? Not really. The premise was great. The idea worthy of exploring... it could have been amazing in every way and yet... what I got was a whole lot of mystical mumbo jumbo, wrapped in awkward metaphors and truly atrocious dialogue. I have never in life been so frustrated with the dialogues in a book before. Perhaps the authoress wanted to keep the aura of mystery going, but instead managed to make the characters who talked seem disjointed and off their rocker.<br /><br />There could have been world building too. That is one of the beauties in post apocalyptic scenarios. There should have been world building.... but instead there were a lot of stupid names for places and people... hardly any descriptions and just random infodump here and there.<br /><br />And let's not even talk about the sex scenes. What scenes and what sex, I ask you. We got a new species and new organs and new names for everything, I get that... but instead of it being sensual and exciting... such erotica as there was in these books felt more like cringing at graphic tentacle porn. Wrapped up in awkward metaphors and mystical mumbo jumbo, of course.<br /><br />Why two stars, then? Because Calanthe. Because screwed up as he is, he's made the books bearable.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2843549426098875456" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_882858272" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/882858272" />
    <a title="Ali" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4891239-ali"><img alt="Ali" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1296267747p2/4891239.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/882858272?book_show_action=true">Mar 15, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ali" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ali" href="/user/show/4891239-ali">Ali</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4891239-ali?shelf=good-read">good-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4891239-ali?shelf=part-of-series">part-of-series</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4891239-ali?shelf=read-and-loved-many-years-ago">read-and-loved-many-years-ago</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer882858272" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10144793091946129312">I read this first many moons ago and was just swept away by the imagery and the writing. It was a dark, fantastical mix of everything I was intrigued by at the time. I consumed the series although I did find the unremitting angst in the latter books far too heavy and tedious.<br/><br/>The Wraeththu were beautiful, long lived and almost unageing. They had magic and mystery. They were sexy and cool, what was there to dislike about them... . :-) <br/><br/>Reflecting on them now with time and experience on my side it w</span>
  <span id="freeText10144793091946129312" style="display:none">I read this first many moons ago and was just swept away by the imagery and the writing. It was a dark, fantastical mix of everything I was intrigued by at the time. I consumed the series although I did find the unremitting angst in the latter books far too heavy and tedious.<br /><br />The Wraeththu were beautiful, long lived and almost unageing. They had magic and mystery. They were sexy and cool, what was there to dislike about them... . :-) <br /><br />Reflecting on them now with time and experience on my side it was my first introduction to the kind of m/m or slash writing that tends to sideline women - here by making the wraeththu hermaphrodites the author didn't even need woman! Women were occasionally mentioned and even I recall given their own way forward, but they weren't very relevant to the Wraththu world. At the time this didn't bother me, I was too busy wallowing in beautiful men/wraththu. Nowadays I can still wallow but I get to feel slightly uneasy as well because, lets face it, Swift and Cal sounded like, acted like and were in essence 'male'. The Wraththu who embraced their more feminine side seemed to be perceived as lesser and weaker, so we're back to sexual stereotyping and assigned 'gender' roles despite the fact that the Wraeththu were meant to be a step forward in evolution.<br /><br />Anyway, interesting book. </span>
  <a data-text-id="10144793091946129312" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('zrBZfTTTF1cXJbLlj7s33RUiZ5NK2Mea4ABqkKXUCc7ZFDWmdTvrWncooe618Gnt3f16BJvX1U0Jpmrm+Y/zeA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('H6m83x0JmIFiRZGeIbgM/OIoHEDry2v7xhW8bOzLFLcIDdAEXOFkjAJIgpUb81LMKvcB1zrEeSwvs7wasJDuAQ==')})
  }

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer14977394744949878361">Storm Constantine (b. 1956) is a British science fiction and fantasy author, primarily known for her <i>Wraeththu</i> series.<br/><br/>Since the late 1980s she has written more than 20 novels, plus several non-fiction books. She is featured in the Goth Bible and is often included in discussions of alternative sexuality and gender in science fiction and fantasy; many of her novels include same-sex relationships or </span>
  <span id="freeText14977394744949878361" style="display:none">Storm Constantine (b. 1956) is a British science fiction and fantasy author, primarily known for her <i>Wraeththu</i> series.<br /><br />Since the late 1980s she has written more than 20 novels, plus several non-fiction books. She is featured in the Goth Bible and is often included in discussions of alternative sexuality and gender in science fiction and fantasy; many of her novels include same-sex relationships or hermaphrodites or other twists of gender. Magic, mysticism and ancient legends (like the Grigori) also figure strongly in her works.<br /><br />In 2003 she launched Immanion Press, based out of Stafford, England. The publishing company publishes not only her own works but those of new writers, as well as well-known genre writers, mainly from the UK.<br /></span>
